<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Falasha_makstyle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Falasha_makstyle.jpg/200px-Falasha_makstyle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Falasha_makstyle.jpg/300px-Falasha_makstyle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Falasha_makstyle.jpg/400px-Falasha_makstyle.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Falasha_makstyle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beta Israel soldier in the <a href="/wiki/Israel_Defense_Forces" title="Israel Defense Forces">Israel Defense Forces</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Beta Israel</b> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Cardo, Shofar, David, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;">בֵּיתֶא יִשְׂרָאֵל</span>‎, <i>Beyte (beyt) Yisrael</i>; <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a>: <span lang="gez-Ethi" xml:lang="gez-Ethi">ቤተ እስራኤል, <i>Bēta 'Isrā'ēl</i></span>, modern <i>Bēte 'Isrā'ēl</i>, <a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Aethiopica" title="Encyclopaedia Aethiopica">EAE</a>: "Betä Ǝsraʾel", "House of Israel" or "Community of Israel"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>), also known as <b>Ethiopian Jews</b> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Cardo, Shofar, David, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;">יְהוּדֵי אֶתְיוֹפְּיָה</span>‎: <i>Yehudey Etyopyah;</i> <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a>: የኢትዮጵያ አይሁድዊ, <i>ye-Ityoppya Ayhudi</i>), are <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> that developed and lived for centuries in the area of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopian Empire</a> that is currently divided between <a href="/wiki/Amhara_Region" title="Amhara Region">Amhara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigray_Region" title="Tigray Region">Tigray</a> <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Ethiopia" title="Regions of Ethiopia">Regions of Ethiopia</a>. Most of these peoples have emigrated to Israel since the late 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beta Israel lived in northern and northwestern Ethiopia, in more than 500 small villages spread over a wide territory, alongside populations that were Muslim and predominantly Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Most of them were concentrated in the area around and to the north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a>, in the Tigray Region among the Wolqayit, Shire and Tselemt, in the Amhara Region of Gonder, and in the Semien Province found in Dembia, Segelt, Quara, and Belesa.</p>
<p>The Beta Israel made renewed contacts with other Jewish communities in the later 20th century. After <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakhic</a> and constitutional discussions, Israeli officials decided on March 14, 1977, that the Israeli <a href="/wiki/Right_of_return#Israel" title="Right of return">Law of Return</a> applied to the Beta Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Israel" title="Cabinet of Israel">Israeli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">American governments</a> mounted <a href="/wiki/Aliyah" title="Aliyah">aliyah</a> operations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> to transport the people to Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> These activities included Operation Brothers in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> between 1979 and 1990 (this includes the major operations <a href="/wiki/Operation_Moses" title="Operation Moses">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Joshua" title="Operation Joshua">Joshua</a>), and in the 1990s from <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a> (which includes <a href="/wiki/Operation_Solomon" title="Operation Solomon">Operation Solomon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isn.ethz.ch_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-isn.ethz.ch-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of 2008, there were 119,300 people of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Jews_in_Israel" title="Ethiopian Jews in Israel">Ethiopian descent in Israel</a>, including nearly 81,000 people born in Ethiopia and about 38,500 native-born Israelis (about 32 percent of the community) with at least one parent born in Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haaretz.com_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-haaretz.com-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1355" data-file-width="895" height="378" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg/250px-Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg/375px-Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg/500px-Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Israel_Defense_Forces_-_First_Ethiopian_Ordnance_Officer_in_Israeli_History.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First IDF ordnance officer of Ethiopian Jewish origin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout its history, the community has been referred to by numerous names. According to tradition the name "Beta Israel" (literally "house of Israel" in <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez)</a> originated in the 4th century CE, when the community refused to convert to Christianity during the rule of Abreha and Atsbeha (identified with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Se%27azana_of_Axum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Se'azana of Axum (page does not exist)">Se'azana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ezana_of_Axum" title="Ezana of Axum">Ezana</a>), the monarchs of the Kingdom of Aksum who embraced Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> This name contrasts with "Beta Kristiyan" (literally "house of Christianity", referring to "church" in Ge'ez).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It did not originally have negative connotations,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Beta_Israel#Dubious" title="Talk:Beta Israel">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> and the community has since used Beta Israel as its official name. Since the 1980s, it has also become the official name used in the scholarly and scientific literature to refer to the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Names_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Names-16">[16]</a></sup> The term <i>Esra'elawi</i> "<a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a>"—which is related to the name Beta Israel—is also used by the community to refer to its members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Names_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Names-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name <i>Ayhud</i> "<a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>" is rarely used in the community, as the Christians had used it as a derogatory term. The community has begun to use it only since strengthening ties with other Jewish communities in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Names_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Names-16">[16]</a></sup> The term <i>Ibrawi</i> "<a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrew</a>" was used to refer to the <i>Chawa</i> (free man) in the community, in contrast to <i>Barya</i> "slave".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The term <i>Oritawi</i> "<a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>-true" was used to refer to the community members; since the 19th century it has been used in opposition to the term <i>Falash Mura</i> (converts).</p>
<p>The major derogatory term <i>Falasha</i> "landless, wanderers" was given to the community by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Yeshaq_I" title="Yeshaq I">Yeshaq I</a> in the 15th century. <i>Zagwe,</i> referring to the <a href="/wiki/Agaw_people" title="Agaw people">Agaw people</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Zagwe_dynasty" title="Zagwe dynasty">Zagwe dynasty</a>, among the original inhabitants of northwest Ethiopia, is considered derogatory since it incorrectly associates the community with the largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religion" title="Traditional African religion">pagan</a> Agaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Names_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Names-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haymanot" title="Haymanot">Haymanot</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="677" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg/220px-%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg/330px-%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg/440px-%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D7%91%D7%A7%D7%AA%D7%AA_%D7%A0%D7%99%D7%93%D7%94_%D7%91%D7%9B%D7%A4%D7%A8_%D7%90%D7%9E%D7%91%D7%95%D7%91%D7%A8_1976.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A niddah hut (Mergem Gogo) at the Jewish village of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ambober&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ambober (page does not exist)">Ambober</a> in northern Ethiopia, 1976.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Haymanot (<a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a>: ሃይማኖት) is the colloquial term of the <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish religion</a> in the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Texts">Texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Mäṣḥafä Kedus</i> (Holy Scriptures) is the name for the religious literature. The language of the writings is <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a>, which is also the liturgical language of the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Church</a>. It is the parent-language of modern <a href="/wiki/Amharic" title="Amharic">Amharic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigrinya_language" title="Tigrinya language">Tigrinya</a>. The holiest book is the Orit (used by the Ethiopian Orthodox Church to denote the word "scripture") which consists of the <a href="/wiki/Octateuch" title="Octateuch">Octateuch</a>: <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Five Books of Moses</a> with <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Joshua" title="Book of Joshua">Joshua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judges" title="Book of Judges">Judges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ruth" title="Book of Ruth">Ruth</a>. The rest of the Bible has secondary importance. Sources are lacking on whether the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Lamentations" title="Book of Lamentations">Book of Lamentations</a> is excluded from the canon, or whether it forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jeremiah" title="Book of Jeremiah">Book of Jeremiah</a> as it does in the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Tewahedo_biblical_canon" title="Orthodox Tewahedo biblical canon">Orthodox Tewahedo biblical canon</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title='"Sources'>citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deuterocanonical_books" title="Deuterocanonical books">Deuterocanonical books</a> that also make up part of the canon are <a href="/wiki/Sirach" title="Sirach">Sirach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judith" title="Book of Judith">Judith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esdras" title="Esdras">Esdras</a> <a href="/wiki/1_Esdras" title="1 Esdras">1</a> and <a href="/wiki/2_Esdras" title="2 Esdras">2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meqabyan" title="Meqabyan">Meqabyan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jubilees" title="Jubilees">Jubilees</a>, Baruch <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Baruch" title="Book of Baruch">1</a> and <a href="/wiki/4_Baruch" title="4 Baruch">4</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Tobit" title="Book of Tobit">Tobit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Enoch" title="Book of Enoch">Enoch</a>, and the testaments of <a href="/wiki/Testament_of_Abraham" title="Testament of Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Testament_of_Isaac" title="Testament of Isaac">Isaac</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Testament_of_Jacob" title="Testament of Jacob">Jacob</a>.</p>
<p>Important non-Biblical writings include: <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nagara_Muse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nagara Muse (page does not exist)">Nagara Muse</a></i> (The Conversation of Moses), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mota_Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mota Aaron (page does not exist)">Mota Aaron</a></i> (Death of Aharon), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mota_Muse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mota Muse (page does not exist)">Mota Muse</a></i> (Death of Moses), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Te%27ezaza_Sanbat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Te'ezaza Sanbat (page does not exist)">Te'ezaza Sanbat</a></i> (Precepts of Sabbath), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Arde%27et&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Arde'et (page does not exist)">Arde'et</a></i> (Students), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gorgorios&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gorgorios (page does not exist)">Gorgorios</a>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=M%C3%A4%E1%B9%A3%E1%B8%A5af%C3%A4_Sa%27atat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mäṣḥafä Sa'atat (page does not exist)">Mäṣḥafä Sa'atat</a></i> (Book of Hours), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Abba_Elias&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Abba Elias (page does not exist)">Abba Elias</a></i> (Father Elija), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=M%C3%A4%E1%B9%A3%E1%B8%A5af%C3%A4_M%C3%A4la%27%C9%99kt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mäṣḥafä Mäla'əkt (page does not exist)">Mäṣḥafä Mäla'əkt</a></i> (Book of Angels), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=M%C3%A4%E1%B9%A3%E1%B8%A5af%C3%A4_Kahan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mäṣḥafä Kahan (page does not exist)">Mäṣḥafä Kahan</a></i> (Book of Priest), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=D%C9%99rsan%C3%A4_Abr%C9%99ham_W%C3%A4sara_B%C3%A4gabs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dərsanä Abrəham Wäsara Bägabs (page does not exist)">Dərsanä Abrəham Wäsara Bägabs</a></i> (Homily on Abraham and Sarah in Egypt), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gadla_Sosna&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gadla Sosna (page does not exist)">Gadla Sosna</a></i> (The Acts of Susanna) and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Baqad%C4%81mi_Gabra_Egzi%27ab%E1%B8%A5%C4%93r&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Baqadāmi Gabra Egzi'abḥēr (page does not exist)">Baqadāmi Gabra Egzi'abḥēr</a></i> (In the Beginning God Created). <i>Zëna Ayhud</i> (<a href="/wiki/Josippon" title="Josippon">Jews Story</a>) and <i>Fālasfā</i> (Philosophers) are two books that are not considered sacred but have had great influence.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayer_house">Prayer house</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Prayer house">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">Synagogue</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolleka.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1228" data-file-width="1818" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Wolleka.jpg/250px-Wolleka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Wolleka.jpg/375px-Wolleka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Wolleka.jpg/500px-Wolleka.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Synagogue in the village of <a href="/wiki/Wolleka" title="Wolleka">Wolleka</a> in Ethiopia</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg/250px-PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg/375px-PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg/500px-PikiWiki_Israel_10703_Architecture_of_Israel.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The synagogue is called <i>masgid</i> (place of worship), also <i>bet maqds</i> (Holy house) or <i>ṣalot bet</i> (Prayer house).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_laws">Dietary laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dietary laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">Kashrut</a></div>
<p>Beta Israel <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">kashrut</a> law is based mainly on the books of <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Leviticus" title="Book of Leviticus">Leviticus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy" title="Book of Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jubilees" title="Book of Jubilees">Jubilees</a>. Permitted and forbidden animals and their signs appear on <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0311.htm#3" rel="nofollow">Leviticus 11:3–8</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0514.htm#4" rel="nofollow">Deuteronomy 14:4–8</a>. Forbidden birds are listed on <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0311.htm#13" rel="nofollow">Leviticus 11:13–23</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0514.htm#12" rel="nofollow">Deuteronomy 14:12–20</a>. Signs of permitted fish are written on <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0311.htm#9" rel="nofollow">Leviticus 11:9–12</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0514.htm#9" rel="nofollow">Deuteronomy 14:9–10</a>. Insects and larvae are forbidden according to <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0311.htm#41" rel="nofollow">Leviticus 11:41–42</a>. <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" title="Waterfowl">Waterfowl</a> are forbidden according to <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0311.htm#46" rel="nofollow">Leviticus 11:46</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gid_hanasheh" title="Gid hanasheh">Gid hanasheh</a> is forbidden per <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0132.htm#33" rel="nofollow">Genesis 32:33</a>. <a href="/wiki/Milk_and_meat_in_Jewish_law" title="Milk and meat in Jewish law">Mixtures of milk and meat</a> are not prepared or eaten but are not banned either: Haymanot interpreted the verses <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0223.htm#19" rel="nofollow">Exodus 23:19</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0234.htm#26" rel="nofollow">Exodus 34:26</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0514.htm#21" rel="nofollow">Deuteronomy 14:21</a> "shalt not seethe a kid in its mother's milk" literally, as in <a href="/wiki/Karaite_Judaism" title="Karaite Judaism">Karaite Judaism</a>. Nowadays, under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a>, mixing dairy products with meat is banned.</p>
<p>Ethiopian Jews were forbidden to eat the food of non-Jews. A <a href="/wiki/Kahen_(Beta_Israel)" title="Kahen (Beta Israel)">Kahen</a> eats only meat he has slaughtered himself, which his hosts prepare both for him and themselves. Beta Israel who broke these taboos were ostracized and had to undergo a purification process. Purification included fasting for one or more days, eating only uncooked chickpeas provided by the Kahen, and ritual purification before entering the village. Unlike other Ethiopians, the Beta Israel do not eat raw meat dishes such as <a href="/wiki/Kitfo" title="Kitfo">kitfo</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gored_gored" title="Gored gored">gored gored</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calendar_and_holidays">Calendar and holidays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Calendar and holidays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Beta Israel calendar is a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a> of 12 months, each 29 or 30 days alternately. Every four years there is a <a href="/wiki/Leap_year#Hebrew_calendar" title="Leap year">leap year</a> which added a full month (30 days). The calendar is a combination of the ancient calendar of Alexandrian Jewry, Book of Jubilees, Book of Enoch, Abu Shaker, and the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_calendar" title="Ethiopian calendar">Ge'ez calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The years are counted according to the counting of Kushta: "1571 to Jesus Christ, 7071 to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Gyptians</a> and 6642 to the Hebrews";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> according to this counting, the year 5771 (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">ה'תשע"א</span>‎‎) in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar" title="Hebrew calendar">Rabbinical Hebrew calendar</a> is the year 7082 in this calendar.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_holiday" title="Jewish holiday">Holidays</a> in the <i>Haymanot</i> (religion)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> are divided into daily, monthly and annually. The annual holidays by month are:</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sigd-27.11.08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1728" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Sigd-27.11.08.jpg/220px-Sigd-27.11.08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Sigd-27.11.08.jpg/330px-Sigd-27.11.08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Sigd-27.11.08.jpg/440px-Sigd-27.11.08.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sigd-27.11.08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kessim" title="Kessim">Kes</a> celebrating the holiday of <a href="/wiki/Sigd" title="Sigd">Sigd</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">Nisan</a>: <i>ba'āl lisan</i> (Nisan holiday – <a href="/wiki/New_Year" title="New Year">New Year</a>) on 1, <i>ṣomä fāsikā</i> (Passover fast) on 14, <i>fāsikā</i> (<a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a>) between 15 – 21 and <i>gadfat</i> (grow fat) or <i>buho</i> (fermented dough) on 22.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iyar" title="Iyar">Iyar</a>: another <i>fāsikā</i> (Second Passover – <a href="/wiki/Pesach_Sheni" title="Pesach Sheni">Pesach Sheni</a>) between 15 – 21.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sivan" title="Sivan">Sivan</a>: <i>ṣomä mã'rar</i> (Harvest fast) on 11 and <i>mã'rar</i> (Harvest – <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">Shavuot</a>) on 12.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tammuz_(Hebrew_month)" title="Tammuz (Hebrew month)">Tammuz</a>: <i>ṣomä tomos</i> (Tammuz fast) between 1 – 10.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Av" title="Av">Av</a>: <i>ṣomä ab</i> (Av fast) between 1 – 17.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elul" title="Elul">Elul</a>: <i>awd amet</i> (Year rotate) on 1, <i>ṣomä lul</i> (Elul fast) between 1 – 9, <i>anākel astar'i</i> (our atonement) on 10 and <i>asartu wasamantu</i> (eighteenth) on 28.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tishrei" title="Tishrei">Tishrei</a>: <i>ba'āl Matqe</i> (blowing holiday – <a href="/wiki/Rosh_Hashanah" title="Rosh Hashanah">Zikhron Trua</a>) on 1, <i>astasreyo</i> (Day of Atonement – <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur" title="Yom Kippur">Yom Kippur</a>) on 10 and <i>ba'āla maṣallat</i> (Tabernacles holiday – <a href="/wiki/Sukkot" title="Sukkot">Sukkot</a>) between 15 – 21.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cheshvan" title="Cheshvan">Cheshvan</a>: holiday for the day <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> saw the face of God on 1, holiday for the reception of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> by the Israelites on 10, fast on 12 and <i>měhlělla</i> (Supplication – <a href="/wiki/Sigd" title="Sigd">Sigd</a>) on 29.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kislev" title="Kislev">Kislev</a>: another <i>ṣomä mã'rar</i> and <i>mã'rar</i> on 11 and 12 respectively.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tevet" title="Tevet">Tevet</a>: <i>ṣomä tibt</i> (Tevet fast) between 1 – 10.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shevat" title="Shevat">Shevat</a>: <i>wamashi brobu</i> on 1.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adar" title="Adar">Adar</a>: <i>ṣomä astēr</i> (Fast of Esther – <a href="/wiki/Fast_of_Esther" title="Fast of Esther">Ta'anit Ester</a>) between 11 – 13.</li>
</ul>
<p>Monthly holidays are mainly memorial days to the annual holiday, these are <i>yačaraqā ba'āl</i> ("new moon festival")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> on the first day of every month, <i>asärt</i> ("ten") on the tenth day to commemorate Yom Kippur, '<i>asrã hulat</i> ("twelve") on the twelfth day to commemorate Shavuot, <i>asrã ammest</i> ("fifteen") on the fifteenth day to commemorate Passover and Sukkot, and <i>ṣomä mälěya</i> a fast on the last day of every month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Daily holidays include the <i>ṣomä säňňo</i> (Monday fast), <i>ṣomä amus</i> (Thursday fast), <i>ṣomä 'arb</i> (Friday fast) and the very holy <i>Sanbat</i> (<a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Sabbath</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habesha_people#Culture" title="Habesha people">Habesha Culture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Beta Israel once spoke <a href="/wiki/Qwara_dialect" title="Qwara dialect">Qwara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kayla_dialect" title="Kayla dialect">Kayla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agaw_languages" title="Agaw languages">Agaw languages</a>. Now they speak <a href="/wiki/Amharic" title="Amharic">Amharic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigrinya_language" title="Tigrinya language">Tigrinya</a>, both <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a>. Their liturgical language is <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a>, also Semitic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Since the 1950s, they have taught <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> in their schools. Those Beta Israel residing in the State of Israel now use <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">Modern Hebrew</a> as a daily language.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oral_traditions">Oral traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Oral traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no independent tradition of origin transmitted over the ages among the Ethiopian Jews. The known Beta Israel versions of the Ethiopian legend of origin take as their basis the account of <a href="/wiki/Menelik_I" title="Menelik I">Menelik</a>'s return to Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Menelik is considered the first <a href="/wiki/Solomonic_dynasty" title="Solomonic dynasty">Solomonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Ethiopia" title="Emperor of Ethiopia">Emperor of Ethiopia</a>, and is traditionally believed to be the son of <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">King Solomon</a> of ancient <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and Makeda, ancient <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Sheba" title="Queen of Sheba">Queen of Sheba</a> (in modern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>). Though all the available traditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> correspond to recent interpretations, they reflect ancient convictions. According to Jon Abbink; three different versions are to be distinguished among the traditions which were recorded from the priests of the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Companions_of_Menelik_from_Jerusalem">Companions of Menelik from Jerusalem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Companions of Menelik from Jerusalem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By versions of this type, the Beta Israel expressed their belief that they were not necessarily descendants of King Solomon, but contemporaries of Solomon and Menelik, originating from the kingdom of Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Migrants_by_the_Egyptian_route">Migrants by the Egyptian route</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Migrants by the Egyptian route">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to these versions, the forefathers of the Beta Israel are supposed to have arrived in Ethiopia coming from the North, independently from Menelik and his company:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Falashas  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] migrated like many of the other <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">sons of Israel</a> to exile in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> after the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Solomon%27s_Temple" title="Solomon's Temple">First Temple</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">Babylonians</a> in 586 BCE the time of the Babylonian exile. This group of people was led by the great priest On. They remained in exile in Egypt for few hundred years until the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra</a>. When she was engaged in war against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_Caesar" title="Augustus Caesar">Augustus Caesar</a> the Jews supported her. When she was defeated, it became dangerous for the small minorities to remain in Egypt and so there was another migration (approximately between 39–31 BCE). Some of the migrants went to <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabia</a> and further to the <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>. Some of them went to the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and continued on their way to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, helped by Egyptian traders who guided them through the desert. Some of them entered Ethiopia through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qwara_province" title="Qwara province">Quara</a> (near the Sudanese border), and some came via <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>.</p>
<p>...Later in time, there was an Abyssinian king named <a href="/wiki/Kaleb_of_Axum" title="Kaleb of Axum">Kaleb</a>, who wished to enlarge his kingdom, so he declared war on the <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Yemen</a> and conquered it. And so, during his reign there arrived another group of Jews to Ethiopia, led by Azonos and <a href="/wiki/Dhu_Nuwas" title="Dhu Nuwas">Phinhas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schoenberger_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schoenberger-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:413–414</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kebra_Nagast">Kebra Nagast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kebra Nagast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Ethiopian history described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Kebra_Nagast" title="Kebra Nagast">Kebra Nagast</a></i> relates that Ethiopians are descendants of Israelite tribes who came to <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ethiopia" title="History of Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> with <a href="/wiki/Menelik_I" title="Menelik I">Menelik I</a>, alleged to be the son of King <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Sheba" title="Queen of Sheba">Queen of Sheba</a> (or Makeda, in the legend) (see <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a10.htm#1" rel="nofollow">1 Kings 10:1–13</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt25b09.htm#1" rel="nofollow">2 Chronicles 9:1–12</a>). The legend relates that Menelik, as an adult, returned to his father in Jerusalem, and later resettled in Ethiopia. He took with him the <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">Ark of the Covenant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> there is no mention that the Queen of Sheba either married or had any sexual relations with King Solomon (although some identify her with the "black and beautiful" in <a href="/wiki/Song_of_Songs" title="Song of Songs">Song of Songs</a> 1:5).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Rather, the narrative records that she was impressed with Solomon's wealth and wisdom, and they exchanged royal gifts, and then she returned to rule her people in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a>. However, the "royal gifts" are interpreted by some as sexual contact. The loss of the Ark is not mentioned in the Bible. <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah" title="Hezekiah">Hezekiah</a> later makes reference to the Ark in <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">2 Kings 19:15</a>.</p>
<p>The <i>Kebra Negast</i> asserts that the Beta Israel are descended from a battalion of men of Judah who fled southward down the Arabian coastal lands from <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> after the breakup of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">Kingdom of Israel</a> into two kingdoms in the 10th century BCE (while King <a href="/wiki/Rehoboam" title="Rehoboam">Rehoboam</a> reigned over Judah).</p>
<p>Although the <i>Kebra Nagast</i> and some traditional Ethiopian histories have stated that <a href="/wiki/Gudit" title="Gudit">Gudit</a> (or "Yudit", Judith; another name given her was "Esato", Esther), a 10th-century usurping queen, was Jewish, some scholars consider that it is unlikely that this was the case. It is more likely, they say, that she was a pagan southerner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> or a usurping Christian Aksumite Queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> However, she clearly supported Jews, since she founded the <a href="/wiki/Zagwe_dynasty" title="Zagwe dynasty">Zagwe dynasty</a>, who governed from around 937 to 1270 CE. According to the <i>Kebra Nagast,</i> Jewish, Christian and pagan kings ruled in harmony at that time. Furthermore, the Zagwe dynasty claimed legitimacy (according to the <i>Kebra Nagast</i>) by saying it was descended from Moses and his Ethiopian wife.</p>
<p>Most of the Beta Israel consider the <i>Kebra Negast</i> to be legend. As its name expresses, "Glory of Kings" (meaning the Christian Aksumite kings), it was written in the 14th century in large part to delegitimize the Zagwe dynasty, to promote instead a rival "Solomonic" claim to authentic Jewish Ethiopian antecedents, and to justify the Christian overthrow of the Zagwe by the "Solomonic" Aksumite dynasty, whose rulers are glorified. The writing of this polemic shows that criticisms of the Aksumite claims of authenticity were current in the 14th century, two centuries after they came to power. Many Beta Israel believe that they are descended from the tribe of Dan.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Most reject the "Solomonic" and "Queen of Sheba" legends of the Aksumites.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tribe_of_Dan">Tribe of Dan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tribe of Dan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To prove the antiquity and authenticity of their claims, the Beta Israel cite the 9th-century CE testimony of <a href="/wiki/Eldad_ha-Dani" title="Eldad ha-Dani">Eldad ha-Dani</a> (the Danite), from a time before the Zagwean dynasty was established. Eldad was a Jewish man of dark skin who appeared in Egypt and created a stir in that Jewish community (and elsewhere in the Mediterranean Jewish communities he visited) with claims that he had come from a Jewish kingdom of pastoralists far to the south. The only language Eldad spoke was a hitherto unknown dialect of Hebrew. Although he strictly followed the Mosaic commandments, his observance differed in some details from Rabbinic halakhah. Some observers thought that he might be a Karaite, although his practice also differed from theirs. He carried Hebrew books that supported his explanations of halakhah. He cited ancient authorities in the scholarly traditions of his own people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eldad said that the Jews of his own kingdom descended from the tribe of Dan (which included the Biblical war-hero <a href="/wiki/Samson" title="Samson">Samson</a>) who had fled the civil war in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">Kingdom of Israel</a> between Solomon's son Rehoboam and <a href="/wiki/Jeroboam" title="Jeroboam">Jeroboam</a> the son of Nebat, and resettled in Egypt. From there they moved southwards up the Nile into Ethiopia. The Beta Israel say this confirms that they are descended from these Danites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Some Beta Israel, however, assert that their Danite origins go back to the time of Moses, when some Danites parted from other Jews right after the Exodus and moved south to Ethiopia. Eldad the Danite speaks of at least three waves of Jewish immigration into his region, creating other Jewish tribes and kingdoms. The earliest wave settled in a remote kingdom of the "tribe of Moses": this was the strongest and most secure Jewish kingdom of all, with farming villages, cities and great wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The Mosaic claims of the Beta Israel, in any case, like those of the Zagwe dynasty, are ancient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other sources tell of many Jews who were brought as prisoners of war from ancient Israel by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I" title="Ptolemy I">Ptolemy I</a> and settled on the border of his kingdom with <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>). Another tradition asserts that the Jews arrived either via the old district of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qwara_province" title="Qwara province">Qwara</a> in northwestern Ethiopia, or via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atbara_River" title="Atbara River">Atbara River</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> tributaries flow into Sudan. Some accounts specify the route taken by their forefathers on their way upriver to the south from Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rabbinical_views">Rabbinical views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Rabbinical views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1323" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg/250px-ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg/375px-ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg/500px-ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChiefRabbinate1922Letter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/he:%D7%A7%D7%95%D7%9C_%D7%A7%D7%95%D7%A8%D7%90_%D7%9E%D7%90%D7%AA_%D7%94%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%AA_%D7%94%D7%A8%D7%90%D7%A9%D7%99%D7%AA_%D7%9C%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%A5_%D7%99%D7%A9%D7%A8%D7%90%D7%9C_(%D7%AA%D7%A8%D7%A4%22%D7%91)" title='s:he:קול קורא מאת הרבנות הראשית לארץ ישראל (תרפ"ב)'>Public appeal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Rabbinate_of_Israel" title="Chief Rabbinate of Israel">Chief Rabbinate of Israel</a> to save the Jews of Ethiopia, 1921, signed by Chief Rabbis <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Isaac_Kook" title="Abraham Isaac Kook">Abraham Isaac Kook</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_Meir" title="Jacob Meir">Jacob Meir</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As mentioned above, the 9th-century Jewish traveler <a href="/wiki/Eldad_ha-Dani" title="Eldad ha-Dani">Eldad ha-Dani</a> claimed the Beta Israel descended from the tribe of Dan. He also reported other Jewish kingdoms around his own or in East Africa during this time. His writings probably represent the first mention of the Beta Israel in Rabbinic literature. Despite some skeptical critics, his authenticity has been generally accepted in current scholarship. His descriptions were consistent and even the originally doubtful rabbis of his time were finally persuaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Specific details may be uncertain; one critic has noted Eldad's lack of detailed reference to Ethiopia's geography and any Ethiopian language, although he claimed the area as his homeland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eldad's was not the only medieval testimony about Jewish communities living far to the south of Egypt, which strengthens the credibility of his account. <a href="/wiki/Obadiah_ben_Abraham_Bartenura" title="Obadiah ben Abraham Bartenura">Obadiah ben Abraham Bartenura</a> wrote in a letter from Jerusalem in 1488:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I myself saw two of them in Egypt. They are dark-skinned...and one could not tell whether they keep the teaching of the <a href="/wiki/Karaite_Judaism" title="Karaite Judaism">Karaites</a>, or of the Rabbis, for some of their practices resemble the Karaite teaching...but in other things they appear to follow the instruction of the Rabbis; and they say they are related to the tribe of Dan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Some Jewish legal authorities have asserted that the Beta Israel are the descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Dan" title="Tribe of Dan">tribe of Dan</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes" title="Ten Lost Tribes">Ten Lost Tribes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> They believe that these people established a Jewish kingdom that lasted for hundreds of years. With the rise of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, schisms arose and three kingdoms competed. Eventually, the <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> Ethiopian kingdoms reduced the Jewish kingdom to a small impoverished section. The earliest authority to rule this way was <a href="/wiki/David_ben_Solomon_ibn_Abi_Zimra" title="David ben Solomon ibn Abi Zimra">David ben Solomon ibn Abi Zimra</a> (1479–1573), who explains in a <a href="/wiki/Responsa" title="Responsa">responsum</a> concerning the status of a Beta Israel slave:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>But those Jews who come from the land of Cush are without doubt from the tribe of Dan, and since they did not have in their midst sages who were masters of the tradition, they clung to the simple meaning of the Scriptures. If they had been taught, however, they would not be irreverent towards the words of our sages, so their status is comparable to a Jewish infant taken captive by non-Jews… And even if you say that the matter is in doubt, it is a commandment to redeem them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1973 Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Ovadia_Yosef" title="Ovadia Yosef">Ovadia Yosef</a>, then the Chief Sephardic Rabbi, based on the Radbaz and other accounts, ruled that the Beta Israel were Jews and should be brought to Israel. He was later joined by a number of other authorities who made similar rulings, including the Chief Ashkenazi Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Goren" title="Shlomo Goren">Shlomo Goren</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some notable <a href="/wiki/Posek" title="Posek">poskim</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Non-Zionism" title="Non-Zionism">non-Zionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi</a> circles, placed a <i>safek</i> "legal doubt" over the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_peoplehood" title="Jewish peoplehood">Jewish peoplehood</a> of the Beta Israel. Such dissenting voices include rabbis <a href="/wiki/Elazar_Shach" title="Elazar Shach">Elazar Shach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yosef_Shalom_Eliashiv" title="Yosef Shalom Eliashiv">Yosef Shalom Eliashiv</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Zalman_Auerbach" title="Shlomo Zalman Auerbach">Shlomo Zalman Auerbach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Feinstein" title="Moshe Feinstein">Moshe Feinstein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Similar doubts were raised within the same circles towards the <a href="/wiki/Bene_Israel" title="Bene Israel">Bene Israel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and to Russian immigrants to Israel during the <a href="/wiki/1990s_Post-Soviet_aliyah" title="1990s Post-Soviet aliyah">1990s Post-Soviet aliyah</a>.</p>
<p>In the 1970s and early 1980s, the Beta Israel were required to undergo a modified conversion ceremony involving immersion in a <a href="/wiki/Mikveh" title="Mikveh">mikveh</a>, a declaration accepting Rabbinic law, and, for men, a "symbolic recircumcision".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Chief Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Avraham_Shapira" title="Avraham Shapira">Avraham Shapira</a> later waived the "symbolic recircumcision" demand, which is only required when the halakhic doubt is significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> More recently, <a href="/wiki/Chief_Rabbi" title="Chief Rabbi">Chief Rabbi</a> <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Amar" title="Shlomo Amar">Shlomo Amar</a> has ruled that descendants of Ethiopian Jews who were <a href="/wiki/Anusim" title="Anusim">forced to convert to Christianity</a> are "unquestionably Jews in every respect".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> With the consent of Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Ovadia_Yosef" title="Ovadia Yosef">Ovadia Yosef</a>, Rabbi Amar ruled that it is forbidden to question the Jewishness of this community, pejoratively called <a href="/wiki/Falash_Mura" title="Falash Mura">Falash Mura</a> in reference to their having converted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Cruciani et al. (2002), <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_A_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup A (Y-DNA)">haplogroup A</a> is the most common paternal lineage among Ethiopian Jews. The clade is carried by around 41% of Beta Israel males, and is primarily associated with <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan</a>-speaking populations. Additionally, around 18% of Ethiopian Jews are bearers of <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E-P2#Distribution" title="Haplogroup E-P2">E-P2 (xM35, xM2)</a>, which today is restricted to a few <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger-Congo</a>-speaking communities in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>. The rest of the Beta Israel mainly belong to haplotypes linked with the <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E-M215_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup E-M215 (Y-DNA)">E1b1b</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup J (Y-DNA)">J</a> haplogroups, which are instead associated with local <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a>-speaking populations in Northeast Africa. Altogether, this suggests that Ethiopian Jews have diverse patrilineages indicative of both Afro-Asiatic and non-Afro-Asiatic origins for this community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cruciani2002_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cruciani2002-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2001 study by the Department of Biological Sciences at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> found a possible genetic similarity between 11 Ethiopian Jews and four <a href="/wiki/Yemenite_Jews" title="Yemenite Jews">Yemenite Jews</a> who took part in the testing. The differentiation statistic and genetic distances for the 11 Ethiopian Jews and four Yemenite Jews tested were quite low, among the smallest of comparisons involving either of these populations. The four Yemenite Jews from this study may be descendants of reverse migrants of Ethiopian origin who crossed Ethiopia to <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>. The study result suggests gene flow between Ethiopia and Yemen as a possible explanation for the closeness. The study also suggests that the gene flow between Ethiopian and Yemenite Jewish populations may not have been direct, but instead could have been between Jewish and non-Jewish populations of both regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2002 study of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (which is passed through only maternal lineage to both men and women) by Thomas <i>et al.</i> showed that the most common mtDNA type found among the Ethiopian Jews sample was present only in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>. This further supported the view that all Ethiopian Beta Israel were of local or Ethiopian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ethiopian Jews' <a href="/wiki/Autosome" title="Autosome">autosomal</a> <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> has been examined in a comprehensive study by Tishkoff et al. (2009) on the genetic affiliations of various populations in Africa. According to the analysis, the Beta Israel showed significant Afro-Asiatic affinities. They also shared some ties with neighboring <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> speakers in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region due to considerable genetic exchanges with these communities over the past 5000 or so years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tishkoff2009_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tishkoff2009-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 study by Behar <i>et al.</i> on the genome-wide structure of Jews observed that the Beta Israel had levels of the Middle Eastern genetic clusters similar to the Semitic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Tigrayans" title="Tigrayans">Tigrayans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amhara_people" title="Amhara people">Amharas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behar2010_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behar2010-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of other DNA studies have been done on the Beta Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2012 study showed that although the Beta Israel more closely resemble the indigenous populations of Ethiopia, they have some distant Jewish ancestry, going back 2000 years. This has resulted in speculation that the community was founded by a few Jewish itinerant traders who moved to Ethiopia, converted locals to Judaism, and married into the local population. This evidence has been used as an explanation as to why the Beta Israel had no idea about the holiday of <a href="/wiki/Hanukkah" title="Hanukkah">Hanukkah</a> until they were resettled in Israel. The holiday commemorates events in the second century BC, long after their ancestors had left Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_views">Scholarly views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Scholarly views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_views">Early views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Early views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early secular scholars considered the Beta Israel to be the direct descendant of Jews who lived in ancient Ethiopia, whether they were the descendants of an Israelite tribe, or converted by Jews living in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, or by the Jewish community in southern Egypt at <a href="/wiki/Elephantine" title="Elephantine">Elephantine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1829, Marcus Louis wrote that the ancestors of the Beta Israel related to the Asmach, which were also called Sembritae ("foreigners"), an Egyptian regiment numbering 240,000 soldiers and mentioned by Greek geographers and historians. The Asmach emigrated or were exiled from <a href="/wiki/Elephantine" title="Elephantine">Elephantine</a> to Kush in the time of <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_I" title="Psamtik I">Psamtik I</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_II" title="Psamtik II">Psamtik II</a> and settled in <a href="/wiki/Sennar_(state)" title="Sennar (state)">Sennar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> It is possible that <a href="/wiki/Shebna" title="Shebna">Shebna</a>'s party from Rabbinic accounts was part of the Asmach.</p>
<p>In the 1930s Jones and Monro argued that the chief Semitic languages of Ethiopia may suggest an antiquity of Judaism in Ethiopia. "There still remains the curious circumstance that a number of Abyssinian words connected with religion, such as the words for <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">Hell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">idol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">purification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alms" title="Alms">alms</a>– are of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> origin. These words must have been derived directly from a Jewish source, for the Abyssinian Church knows the scriptures only in a Ge'ez version made from the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Pankhurst_(academic)" title="Richard Pankhurst (academic)">Richard Pankhurst</a> summarized the various theories offered about their origins as of 1950 that the first members of this community were</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>(1) converted Agaws, (2) Jewish immigrants who intermarried with Agaws, (3) immigrant Yemeni Arabs who had converted to Judaism, (4) immigrant Yemeni Jews, (5) Jews from Egypt, and (6) successive waves of Yemeni Jews. Traditional Ethiopian savants, on the one hand, have declared that 'We were Jews before we were Christians', while more recent, well-documented, Ethiopian hypotheses, notably by two Ethiopian scholars, Dr Taddesse Tamrat and Dr Getachew Haile...put much greater emphasis on the manner in which Christians over the years converted to the Falasha faith, thus showing that the Falashas were culturally an Ethiopian sect, made up of ethnic Ethiopians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pankhurst-567_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pankhurst-567-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s_and_early_1990s">1980s and early 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 1980s and early 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to Jacqueline Pirenne, numerous <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Sabaeans</a> left south Arabia and crossed over the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> to Ethiopia to escape from the Assyrians, who had devastated the kingdoms of Israel and Judah in the 8th and 7th centuries BCE. She says that a second major wave of Sabeans crossed over to Ethiopia in the 6th and 5th centuries BCE to escape <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II" title="Nebuchadnezzar II">Nebuchadnezzar II</a>. This wave also included Jews fleeing from the Babylonian takeover of Judah. In both cases the Sabeans are assumed to have departed later from Ethiopia to Yemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Menachem Waldman, a major wave of emigration from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Kingdom of Judah</a> to Kush and Abyssinia dates to the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Siege_of_Jerusalem" title="Assyrian Siege of Jerusalem">Assyrian Siege of Jerusalem</a>, in the beginning of the 7th century BCE. <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">Rabbinic</a> accounts of the siege assert that only about 110,000 Judeans remained in Jerusalem under King <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah" title="Hezekiah">Hezekiah</a>'s command, whereas about 130,000 Judeans led by <a href="/wiki/Shebna" title="Shebna">Shebna</a> had joined <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">Sennacherib</a>'s campaign against <a href="/wiki/Taharqa#Biblical_references" title="Taharqa">Tirhakah</a>, king of Kush. Sennacherib's campaign failed and Shebna's army was lost "at the mountains of darkness", suggestively identified with the <a href="/wiki/Semien_Mountains" title="Semien Mountains">Semien Mountains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1987 <a href="/wiki/Steve_Kaplan" title="Steve Kaplan">Steve Kaplan</a> wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Although we don't have a single fine ethnographic research on Beta Israel, and the recent history of this tribe has received almost no attention by researchers, every one who writes about the Jews of Ethiopia feels obliged to contribute his share to the ongoing debate about their origin. Politicians and journalists, Rabbis and political activists, not a single one of them withstood the temptation to play the role of the historian and invent a solution for this riddle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Richard Pankhurst summarized the state of knowledge on the subject in 1992 as follows: "The early origins of the Falashas are shrouded in mystery, and, for lack of documentation, will probably remain so for ever."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pankhurst-567_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pankhurst-567-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_views">Recent views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Recent views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By 1994 modern scholars of Ethiopian history and Ethiopian Jews generally supported one of two conflicting hypotheses for the origin of the Beta Israel, as outlined by Kaplan:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan94_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan94-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>An ancient Jewish origin, together with conservation of some ancient Jewish traditions by the Ethiopian Church. Kaplan identifies Simon D. Messing, David Shlush, Michael Corinaldi, Menachem Waldman, <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Elon" title="Menachem Elon">Menachem Elon</a> and David Kessler as supporters of this hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan94_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan94-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>A late <a href="/wiki/Ethnogenesis" title="Ethnogenesis">ethnogenesis</a> of the Beta Israel between the 14th to 16th centuries, from a sect of Ethiopian Christians who took on Biblical Old Testament practices, and came to identify as Jews. Steven Kaplan supports this hypothesis, and lists with him G. J. Abbink, Kay K. Shelemay, Taddesse Tamrat and James A. Quirin. Quirin differs from his fellow researchers in the weight he assigns to an ancient Jewish element which the Beta Israel have conserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan94_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan94-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_independence_.284th_century_.E2.80.93_1632.29">Political independence (4th century – 1632)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Political independence (4th century – 1632)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Semien" title="Kingdom of Semien">Kingdom of Semien</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="355" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/eb/The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG/220px-The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/eb/The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG/330px-The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/eb/The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Area_Of_Beta_Israel_Jewish_Community.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The historical region of Beta Israel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the Beta Israel tradition, the Jewish kingdom of Beta Israel, later called the kingdom of Gondar, was initially established after <a href="/wiki/Ezana_of_Axum" title="Ezana of Axum">Ezana</a> was crowned as the Emperor of Axum (in 325 CE). Ezana, who was educated in his childhood by the missionary <a href="/wiki/Frumentius" title="Frumentius">Frumentius</a>, declared Christianity as the religion of the Ethiopian empire after he was crowned. The inhabitants who practiced <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and refused to convert to Christianity began revolting – this group was referred to as "Beta Israel" by the emperor. Following civil war between the Jewish population and the Christian population the Beta Israel appear to have forged an independent state, either in northern western Ethiopia or the eastern region of Northern Sudan. By the 13th century, the Beta Israel have already moved to the more easily defensible mountains to the northwest of the Christianized region of the plains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The kingdom was located in the <a href="/wiki/Semien_Mountains" title="Semien Mountains">Semien Mountains</a> region and the Dembia region – situated to the north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> and south of the <a href="/wiki/Tekez%C3%A9_River" title="Tekezé River">Tekezé River</a>. They made their main city at Gondar, crowned their first king, Phineas, a descendent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kohen_Gadol" title="Kohen Gadol">Jewish High Priest</a> <a href="/wiki/Zadok" title="Zadok">Zadok</a>, and started a period of territorial expansion eastward and southward.</p>
<p>During the mid-9th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" title="Aksumite Empire">empire of Aksum</a> began a new expansion, which led to an armed conflict between the Empire forces and the Beta Israel forces. The Beta Israel kingdom under King Gideon the fourth managed to defeat the Axum forces. During the battle king Gideon was killed. As a result, Gideon's daughter Judith inherited the kingdom from her father and took command.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg/220px-Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg/330px-Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg/440px-Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Small_Steles_near_Aksum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Judith's Field": an area full of ruins of destroyed buildings which according to tradition were ruined by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Gudit" title="Gudit">Queen Judith</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Queen Judith signed a pact with the <a href="/wiki/Agaw_people" title="Agaw people">Agaw</a> tribes which were <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagans</a>. Around 960, The large tribal confederation led by Queen Judith, which included both forces of the Agaw tribes and the Beta Israel forces, invaded the capital of <a href="/wiki/Axum" title="Axum">Axum</a> and conquered and destroyed the city of <a href="/wiki/Axum" title="Axum">Axum</a> (including many churches and monasteries which were burned and destroyed) and imposed the Jewish rule over Axum. In addition, the Axumite throne was snatched and the forces of Queen Judith sacked and burned the <a href="/wiki/Debre_Damo#The_monastery" title="Debre Damo">Debre Damo monastery</a> which at the time was a treasury and a prison for the male relatives of the emperor of Ethiopia, killing all of the potential heirs of the emperor.</p>
<p>The Golden Age of the Beta Israel kingdom took place, according to the Ethiopian tradition, between the years 858–1270, in which the Jewish kingdom flourished. During that period the world Jewry heard for the first time the stories of <a href="/wiki/Eldad_ha-Dani" title="Eldad ha-Dani">Eldad ha-Dani</a> who either visited the kingdom or heard many accounts of it in his own Jewish kingdom of pastoralists, which may have been located in the Sudan (since he speaks of the Mosaic kingdom lying on "the other side of the rivers of Ethiopia" in remote mountains). Even <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_of_Tudela" title="Benjamin of Tudela">Benjamin of Tudela</a> mention an independent Ethiopian Jewish kingdom in the writings from that period. This period ends with the rise of the Christian <a href="/wiki/Solomonic_dynasty" title="Solomonic dynasty">Solomonic dynasty</a> – In 1270 the Christian <a href="/wiki/Solomonic_dynasty" title="Solomonic dynasty">Solomonic dynasty</a> was "restored" after the crowning of a monarch who claimed descent from the single royal prince who managed to escape Queen Judith's uprising. For the next three centuries, the Solomonic dynasty emperors conducted several long ongoing series of armed confrontations with the Jewish kingdom.</p>
<p>In 1329, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Ethiopia" title="Emperor of Ethiopia">Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Amda_Seyon_I" title="Amda Seyon I">Amda Seyon</a> campaigned in the northwest provinces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semien_province" title="Semien province">Semien</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wegera" title="Wegera">Wegera</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tselemt_province&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tselemt province (page does not exist)">Tselemt</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tsegede_province&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tsegede province (page does not exist)">Tsegede</a>, in which many had been converting to <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and where the Beta Israel had been gaining prominence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PankBord40_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-PankBord40-78">[78]</a></sup> He sent troops there to fight people "like Jews" (<a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a> ከመ:አይሁድ <i>kama ayhūd</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc553_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc553-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Yeshaq_I" title="Yeshaq I">Emperor Yeshaq</a> (1414–1429) who invaded the Jewish kingdom, annexed it and began to exert religious pressure. Yeshaq divided the occupied territories of the Jewish kingdom into three provinces which were controlled by commissioners appointed by him. He reduced the Jews' social status below that of Christians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc553_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc553-79">[79]</a></sup> and forced the Jews to convert or lose their land. It would be given away as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rist_(tenure)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rist (tenure) (page does not exist)">rist</a></i>, a type of land qualification that rendered it forever inheritable by the recipient and not transferable by the Emperor. Yeshaq decreed, "He who is baptized in the Christian religion may inherit the land of his father, otherwise let him be a <i>Falāsī</i>." This may have been the origin for the term "Falasha" (<i>falāšā</i>, "wanderer," or "landless person").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc553_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc553-79">[79]</a></sup> This term is considered derogatory to Ethiopian Jews.</p>
<p>By 1450 the Jewish kingdom managed to annex back the territories it lost beforehand and began preparing to fight the armies of the emperor. The Beta Israel forces invaded the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopian Empire</a> in 1462 but lost the campaign and many of its military forces were killed. Later on the forces of the Ethiopian emperor invaded the kingdom in the region of <a href="/wiki/Begemder" title="Begemder">Begemder</a> and massacred many of the Jews in that region throughout a period of seven years. The Emperor Yacob Zara (reigned 1434–1468) even proudly added the title "Exterminator of the Jews" to his name.
