Although the area of the kingdom became significantly smaller afterwards, the Jews were able to eventually restore their mountain kingdom.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg/220px-Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg/330px-Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg/440px-Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethiopian_highlands_01_mod.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Ras_Dashen" title="Ras Dashen">Ras Dashen</a> area in Ethiopia which used to be part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Semien" title="Kingdom of Semien">Jewish kingdom</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between the years 1529 until 1543 the Muslim <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a> armies with the assistance of forces from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopian%E2%80%93Adal_War" title="Ethiopian–Adal War">invaded and fought the Ethiopian Empire</a> and came close to extinguishing the ancient realm of Ethiopia, and converting all of its subjects to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. During that time period the Jews made a pact with the Ethiopian Empire. The leaders of the Kingdom of Beta Israel changed their alliance during the war and began supporting the Muslim Adal Sultanate armies. However, the Adal Sultanate armies felt strong enough to ignore this offer of support and killed many of its members. As a result, the leaders of the Beta Israel kingdom turned to the Ethiopian empire and their allies, and continued the fight against them. They conquered different regions of the Jewish kingdom, severely damaged its economy and requested their assistance in winning back the regions lost to the Adal Sultanate. The forces of the Ethiopian empire did succeed eventually in conquering the Muslims and freed Ethiopia from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmed_Gragn" title="Ahmed Gragn">Ahmed Gragn</a>. Nevertheless, the Ethiopian Christian empire decided to declare war against the Jewish Kingdom, giving as their justification the Jewish leaders' change of positions during the Ethiopian–Adal War. With the assistance of Portuguese forces from the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Order of the Jesuits</a>, the Ethiopian empire under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Gelawdewos" title="Gelawdewos">Emperor Gelawdewos</a> invaded the Jewish kingdom and executed the Jewish king Joram. As a result of this battle, the areas of the kingdom became significantly smaller and included now only the region of the <a href="/wiki/Semien_Mountains" title="Semien Mountains">Semien Mountains</a>.</p>
<p>In the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Rabbi" title="Chief Rabbi">Chief Rabbi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, Rabbi <a href="/wiki/David_ben_Solomon_ibn_Abi_Zimra" title="David ben Solomon ibn Abi Zimra">David ben Solomon ibn Abi Zimra</a> (Radbaz) proclaimed that in terms of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a> (Jewish legal code), the Ethiopian community was certainly Jewish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the execution of King Joram, King Radi became the leader of the Beta Israel kingdom. King Radi also fought against the Ethiopian Empire which at that period of time was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Menas_of_Ethiopia" title="Menas of Ethiopia">Emperor Menas</a>. The forces of the Jewish kingdom managed to conquer the area south of the kingdom and strengthened their defenses in the Semien Mountains. The battles against the forces of emperor Menas were successful as the Ethiopian empire forces were eventually defeated.</p>
<p>During the reign of emperor <a href="/wiki/Sarsa_Dengel" title="Sarsa Dengel">Sarsa Dengel</a> the Jewish kingdom was invaded and the forces of the Ethiopian empire besieged the kingdom. The Jews survived the siege, but at the end of the siege the King Goshen was executed and many of his soldiers as well as many other Beta Israel members committed mass suicides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Susenyos_I" title="Susenyos I">Susenyos I</a> the Ethiopian empire waged war against the Jewish kingdom and managed to conquer the entire kingdom and annex it to the Ethiopian empire by 1627.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gondar_period_.281632.E2.80.931855.29">Gondar period (1632–1855)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Gondar period (1632–1855)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gonder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="682" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Gonder.jpg/220px-Gonder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Gonder.jpg/330px-Gonder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Gonder.jpg/440px-Gonder.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gonder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The castle of <a href="/wiki/Fasilides" title="Fasilides">Emperor Fasilides</a>, who ruled Ethiopia from 1632 to 1667, was built by many Ethiopian Jews.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the Beta Israel autonomy in Ethiopia ended in the 1620s, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Susenyos_I" title="Susenyos I">Susenyos I</a> confiscated their lands, sold many people into slavery and forcibly <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptized</a> others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc554_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc554-82">[82]</a></sup> In addition, Jewish writings and religious books were burned and the practice of any form of Jewish <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> was forbidden in Ethiopia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As a result of this period of oppression, much traditional Jewish culture and practice was lost or changed.</p>
<p>Nonetheless, the Beta Israel community appears to have continued to flourish during this period. The capital of Ethiopia, <a href="/wiki/Gondar" title="Gondar">Gondar</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Dembiya" title="Dembiya">Dembiya</a>, was surrounded by Beta Israel lands. The Beta Israel served as craftsmen, masons, and carpenters for the Emperors from the 16th century onwards. Such roles had been shunned by Ethiopians as lowly and less honorable than farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc554_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc554-82">[82]</a></sup> According to contemporary accounts by European visitors: Portuguese merchants and diplomats, French, British and other travellers, the Beta Israel numbered about one million persons in the 17th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These accounts also recounted that some knowledge of Hebrew persisted among the people in the 17th century. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manoel_de_Almeida" title="Manoel de Almeida">Manoel de Almeida</a>, a Portuguese diplomat and traveller of the day, wrote that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There were Jews in Ethiopia from the first. Some of them were converted to the law of Christ Our Lord; others persisted in their blindness and formerly possessed many wide territories, almost the whole Kingdom of Dambea and the provinces of Ogara and Seman. This was when the [Christian] empire was much larger, but since the [pagan and Muslim] Gallas have been pressing in upon them [from the east and south], the Emperors have pressed in upon them [i.e., the Jews to the west?] much more and took Dambea and Ogara from them by force of arms many years ago. In Seman, however, they defended themselves with great determination, helped by the position and the ruggedness of their mountains. Many rebels ran away and joined them till the present Emperor Setan Sequed [throne name of Susneyos], who in his 9th year fought and conquered the King Gideon and in his 19th year attacked Samen and killed Gideon. ... The majority and the flower of them were killed in various attacks and the remainder surrendered or dispersed in different directions. Many of them received holy baptism but nearly all were still as much Jews as they had been before. There are many of the latter in Dambea and in various regions; they live by weaving cloth and by making zargunchos [spears], ploughs and other iron articles, for they are great smiths. Between the Emperor’s kingdoms and the Cafres [Negroes] who live next to the Nile outside imperial territory, mingled together with each other are many more of these Jews who are called Falashas here. The Falashas or Jews are ... of [Arabic] race [and speak] Hebrew, though it is very corrupt. They have their Hebrew Bibles and sing the psalms in their synagogues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The sources of De Almeida's knowledge are not spelled out, but they at least reflect contemporary views. His comments regarding the Hebrew knowledge of the Beta Israel of that time is very significant: it could not have come from recent intercourse with Jews elsewhere, so it indicates deep antiquity to Beta Israel traditions, at least at that time, before their literature was taken away from them and demolished by the later conquering Christians. (The more sceptical school of historians, whose views are discussed above, deny that the Ethiopian Jews ever knew Hebrew; they certainly have no Hebrew texts remaining, and have been forced in recent centuries to use the Christian "Old Testament" in Ge'ez after their own literature was destroyed.) It is also of interest that he mentions more Jewish communities dwelling beyond Ethiopia in the Sudan. As so often in such medieval hearsay accounts, however, loose claims are made that may not be accurate. The Beta Israel were not predominantly of the Arabic race, for instance, but he may have meant the term loosely or meant that they also knew Arabic.</p>
<p>The isolation of the Beta Israel community in Ethiopia, and their continuing use of some Hebrew, was also reported by the Scottish explorer <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce" title="James Bruce">James Bruce</a> who published his travelogue <i>Travels to Discover the Source of the Nile</i> in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> in 1790.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:V05p329001_Falashas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="438" data-file-width="760" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/V05p329001_Falashas.jpg/275px-V05p329001_Falashas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/V05p329001_Falashas.jpg/413px-V05p329001_Falashas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/V05p329001_Falashas.jpg/550px-V05p329001_Falashas.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:V05p329001_Falashas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Beta Israel village of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Balankab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Balankab (page does not exist)">Balankab</a>, from H. A. Stern, <i>Wanderings Among the Falashas in Abyssinia</i>, 1862</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Beta Israel lost their relative economic advantage in the late 18th and early 19th centuries, during the <a href="/wiki/Zemene_Mesafint" title="Zemene Mesafint">Zemene Mesafint</a>, a period of recurring civil strife. Although the capital was nominally in Gondar during this time period, the decentralization of government and dominance by regional capitals resulted in a decline and exploitation of Beta Israel by local rulers. No longer was there a strong central government interested in and capable of protecting them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc554_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc554-82">[82]</a></sup> During this period, the Jewish religion was effectively lost for some forty years, before being restored in the 1840s by <i>Abba</i> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Widdaye&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Widdaye (page does not exist)">Widdaye</a>, the preeminent monk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qwara_province" title="Qwara province">Qwara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyc554_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc554-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The contemporary history of the Beta Israel community begins with the reunification of Ethiopia in the mid-19th century during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Tewodros_II" title="Tewodros II">Tewodros II</a>. At that time, the Beta Israel population was estimated at between 200,000 to 350,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_missions_and_the_Rabbinical_reformation">Christian missions and the Rabbinical reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Christian missions and the Rabbinical reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Church%27s_Ministry_Among_Jewish_People" title="Church's Ministry Among Jewish People">Church's Ministry Among Jewish People</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethiopie-falasha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="600" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ethiopie-falasha.jpg/220px-Ethiopie-falasha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ethiopie-falasha.jpg/330px-Ethiopie-falasha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ethiopie-falasha.jpg/440px-Ethiopie-falasha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethiopie-falasha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Regions in which the Beta Israel community has lived in modern times</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite occasional contacts in an earlier stage, the West only became well-aware of the existence of the Beta Israel community when they came in contact through the <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a> of the "<a href="/wiki/Church%27s_Ministry_Among_Jewish_People" title="Church's Ministry Among Jewish People">London Society for Promoting Christianity Amongst the Jews</a>" which specialized in the conversion of Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The organization began its operating in Ethiopia in 1859. The Protestant missionaries, who worked under the direction of a converted Jew named Henry Aaron Stern, converted many of the Beta Israel community to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. Between 1859 and 1922, about 2,000 Beta Israel members converted to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Orthodox Christianity</a> (they did not convert to <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> due to an agreement the Protestant missionaries had with the government of Ethiopia). The relatively low amount of conversions is partly explained by the strong reaction to the conversions from religious leadership of the Beta Israel community<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The Beta Israel members who were converted to Christianity are known today as "Falash Mura".</p>
<p>The Protestant missionaries activities in Ethiopia provoked European Jewry. As a result, several European rabbis proclaimed that they recognized the Jewishness of the Beta Israel community, and eventually in 1868 the organization "<a href="/wiki/Alliance_Isra%C3%A9lite_Universelle" title="Alliance Israélite Universelle">Alliance Israélite Universelle</a>" decided to send the Jewish-French <a href="/wiki/Oriental_studies" title="Oriental studies">Orientalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hal%C3%A9vy" title="Joseph Halévy">Joseph Halévy</a> to Ethiopia in order to study the conditions of the Ethiopian Jews. Upon his return to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, Halévy made a very favorable report of the Beta Israel community in which he called for world Jewish community to save the Ethiopian Jews, to establish Jewish schools in Ethiopia, and even suggested to bring thousands of Beta Israel members to settle in <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Syria" title="Ottoman Syria">Ottoman Syria</a> (a dozen of years before the actual establishment of the first Zionist organization).</p>
<p>Nevertheless, after a brief period in which the media coverage generated a great interest in the Beta Israel community, the interest among the Jewish communities world wide declined. This happened mainly because serious doubts still remained about the Jewishness of the Beta Israel community and because the Alliance Israélite Universelle organization did not comply with Halévy's recommendations<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>Between 1888 and 1892, northern Ethiopia experienced a <a href="/wiki/Famines_in_Ethiopia" title="Famines in Ethiopia">devastating famine</a>. The famine was caused by <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a> that killed the majority of all <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> (see <a href="/wiki/1890s_African_rinderpest_epizootic" title="1890s African rinderpest epizootic">1890s African rinderpest epizootic</a>). Conditions worsened with <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> outbreaks (1889–92), a <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> epidemic, and a major <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> epidemic (1889–90).</p>
<p>About one-third of the Ethiopian population died during that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> It is estimated that between a half to two-thirds of the Beta Israel community died during that period.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Faitlovich.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="282" data-file-width="354" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Jacques_Faitlovich.jpg/250px-Jacques_Faitlovich.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/Jacques_Faitlovich.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Faitlovich.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dr. <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Faitlovitch" title="Jacques Faitlovitch">Jacques Faitlovitch</a> during a visit of Ethiopian Jewish children in his Tel-Aviv home, 1 May 1955</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The myth of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes" title="Ten Lost Tribes">lost tribes</a> in Ethiopia intrigued <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Faitlovitch" title="Jacques Faitlovitch">Jacques Faitlovitch</a>, a former student of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hal%C3%A9vy" title="Joseph Halévy">Joseph Halévy</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecole_des_Hautes_Etudes" title="Ecole des Hautes Etudes">Ecole des Hautes Etudes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>. In 1904 Faitlovitch decided to lead a new mission in northern Ethiopia. Faitlovitch obtained funding from the Jewish philanthropist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmond_de_Rothschild" title="Edmond de Rothschild">Edmond de Rothschild</a>, traveled and lived among the Ethiopian Jews. In addition, Faitlovitch managed to disrupt the efforts of the Protestant missionaries to convert the Ethiopian Jews, who at the time attempted to persuade the Ethiopian Jews that all the Jews in the world believe in <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> between the years 1905–1935, he brought out 25 young Ethiopian Jewish boys, whom he planted in the Jewish communities of Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> for example Salomon Yeshaq,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Taamerat Ammanuel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Abraham Adgeh,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Yona Bogale,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and Tadesse Yacob.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following his visit in Ethiopia, Faitlovitch created an international committee for the Beta Israel community, popularized the awareness of their existence through his book "Notes de voyage chez les Falashas", and raised funds to enable the establishment of schools in their villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1908, the chief rabbis of 45 countries made a joint statement officially declaring that Ethiopian Jews were indeed Jewish.</p>
<p>The Jewishness of the Beta Israel community became openly supported amongst the majority of the European Jewish communities during the early 20th century.</p>
<p>In 1921 <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Isaac_Kook" title="Abraham Isaac Kook">Abraham Isaac Kook</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_rabbi" title="Chief rabbi">chief rabbi</a> of the British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Palestine" title="Mandate for Palestine">Mandate for Palestine</a>, recognized the Beta Israel community as Jews.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Italian_period.2C_World_War_II_and_the_post_war_period">The Italian period, World War II and the post war period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: The Italian period, World War II and the post war period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Second Italo-Abyssinian War</a></div>
<p>In 1935 armed forces of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861–1946)">Kingdom of Italy</a> headed by the fascist leader <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">invaded and occupied</a> Ethiopia. Ethiopia officially surrendered in 1936.</p>
<p>The Italian regime showed hostility towards the Jews of Ethiopia. The racial laws which were enacted in Italy were also applied to Italian East Africa. Mussolini attempted to reach an agreement with Britain which would recognize Italian East Africa, during which Mussolini proposed to solve the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_problem" title="Jewish problem">Jewish problem</a>" in Europe and in <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> by resettling the Jews in the north-west Ethiopian districts of <a href="/wiki/Gojjam" title="Gojjam">Gojjam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Begemder" title="Begemder">Begemder</a> along with the Beta Israel community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The proposed Jewish state was to be federally united with the Italian Empire. Mussolini's plan was never implemented.</p>
<p>When the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" title="State of Israel">State of Israel</a> was established in 1948 many Ethiopian Jews began contemplating immigrating to Israel. Nevertheless, the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie" title="Haile Selassie">Haile Selassie</a> refused to grant the Ethiopian Jewish population permission to leave his empire.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_illegal_emigration_and_the_official_Israeli_recognition">Early illegal emigration and the official Israeli recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Early illegal emigration and the official Israeli recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Between the years 1965 and 1975 a relatively small group of Ethiopian Jews immigrated to Israel. The Beta Israel immigrants in that period were mainly a very few men who had studied and come to Israel on a tourist visa and then remained in the country illegally.</p>
<p>Some supporters in Israel who recognized their Jewishness decided to assist them. These supporters began organizing associations, including one under the direction of Ovadia Hazzi, a Yemeni Jew and former sergeant in the Israeli army who married a wife from the Beta Israel community after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Some of the illegal immigrants managed to regularize their status with the Israeli authorities through the assistance of these support associations. Some agreed to "convert" to Judaism, which helped them to regularize their personal status and thus remain in Israel. Those who had regularized their status often brought their families to Israel as well.</p>
<p>In 1973, Ovadia Hazzi officially raised the question of the Jewishness of the Beta Israel to the Israeli Sephardi Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Ovadia_Yosef" title="Ovadia Yosef">Ovadia Yosef</a>. The rabbi, who cited a rabbinic ruling from the 16th century <a href="/wiki/David_ben_Solomon_ibn_Abi_Zimra" title="David ben Solomon ibn Abi Zimra">David ben Solomon ibn Abi Zimra</a> and asserted that the Beta Israel are descended from the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes" title="Ten Lost Tribes">lost tribe</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Dan" title="Tribe of Dan">Dan</a>, acknowledged their Jewishness in February 1973. This ruling was initially rejected by the Ashkenazi Chief Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Goren" title="Shlomo Goren">Shlomo Goren</a>, who eventually changed his opinion on the matter in 1974.</p>
<p>In April 1975, the Israeli government of <a href="/wiki/Yitzhak_Rabin" title="Yitzhak Rabin">Yitzhak Rabin</a> officially accepted the Beta Israel as Jews, for the purpose of the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Return" title="Law of Return">Law of Return</a> (an Israeli act that grants all the Jews in the world the right to immigrate to Israel).</p>
<p>Later on, Israeli Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Begin" title="Menachem Begin">Menachem Begin</a> obtained clear rulings from Chief Sephardi Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Ovadia_Yosef" title="Ovadia Yosef">Ovadia Yosef</a> that they were descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes" title="Ten Lost Tribes">Ten Lost Tribes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Chief_Rabbinate_of_Israel" title="Chief Rabbinate of Israel">Chief Rabbinate of Israel</a> did however initially require them to undergo <i>pro forma</i> <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">Jewish conversions</a>, to remove any doubt as to their Jewish status.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopian_Civil_War">Ethiopian Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ethiopian Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Civil_War" title="Ethiopian Civil War">Ethiopian Civil War</a></div>
<p>After a period of civil unrest on September 12, 1974, a pro-communist military <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">junta</a>, known as the "<a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a>" ("committee") seized power after ousting the emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie_I" title="Haile Selassie I">Haile Selassie I</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a> installed a government which was socialist in name and military in style. Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Mengistu_Haile_Mariam" title="Mengistu Haile Mariam">Mengistu Haile Mariam</a> assumed power as head of state and Derg chairman. Mengistu's years in office were marked by a totalitarian-style government and the country's massive militarization, financed by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the Eastern Bloc, and assisted by <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>. <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">Communism</a> was officially adopted by the new regime during the late 1970s and early 1980s.</p>
<p>As a result, the new regime gradually began to embrace anti-religious and anti-Israeli positions as well as showing hostility towards the Jews of Ethiopia.</p>
<p>Towards the mid-1980s Ethiopia underwent a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1984%E2%80%931985_famine_in_Ethiopia" title="1984–1985 famine in Ethiopia">series of famines</a>, exacerbated by adverse <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_wars" title="Civil wars">civil wars</a>, which eventually resulted in the deaths of hundreds of thousands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> As a result, the lives of hundreds of thousands of Ethiopians, including the Beta Israel community, became untenable and a large part tried to escape the war and the famine by fleeing to neighboring <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>.</p>
<p>Concern for the fate of the Ethiopian Jews and fear for their well-being contributed eventually to the Israeli government's official recognition of the Beta Israel community as Jews in 1975, for the purpose of the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Return" title="Law of Return">Law of Return</a>. Civil war in Ethiopia prompted the Israeli government to airlift most of the Beta Israel population in Ethiopia to Israel in several covert military rescue operations which took place from the 1980s until the early 1990s (see section below). At the start of 1990, Israel provided military assistance to the Derg regime in exchange for the trouble-free exit of the Beta Israel population.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emigration_to_Israel">Emigration to Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Emigration to Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beta_Israel_Exodus">Beta Israel Exodus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Beta Israel Exodus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The emigration to Israel of the Beta Israel community was officially banned by the Communist <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a> government of Ethiopia during the 1980s, although it is now known that <a href="/wiki/Mengistu_Haile_Mariam" title="Mengistu Haile Mariam">General Menghistu</a> collaborated with Israel to receive money and arms in exchange for allowing the Beta Israel safe passage during <a href="/wiki/Operation_Moses" title="Operation Moses">Operation Moses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Other Beta Israel who did not participate in either Operations or Solomon sought alternative ways of immigration, via Sudan or Kenya.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Late 1979 – beginning of 1984</b> – aliyah activists and <a href="/wiki/Mossad" title="Mossad">Mossad</a> agents operating in Sudan called the Jews to come to Sudan, and told them that, from Sudan via Europe they would be taken to Israel. Posing as Christian Ethiopian refugees from the Ethiopian Civil War, Jews began to arrive in the refugee camps in Sudan. Most Jews came from <a href="/wiki/Tigray_Province" title="Tigray Province">Tigray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolqayt" title="Wolqayt">Wolqayt</a>, regions that were controlled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TPLF" title="TPLF">TPLF</a>, who often escorted them to the Sudanese border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Small groups of Jews were brought out of Sudan in a clandestine operation that continued until an Israeli newspaper exposed the operation and brought it to a halt stranding Beta Israels in the Sudanese camps. In 1981, the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Defense_League" title="Jewish Defense League">Jewish Defense League</a> protested the "lack of action" to rescue Ethiopian Jews by taking over the main offices of <a href="/wiki/HIAS" title="HIAS">HIAS</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1983 – March 28, 1985</b> – In 1983 the governor of <a href="/wiki/Begemder" title="Begemder">Gondar</a> region, Major <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Melaku_Teferra&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Melaku Teferra (page does not exist)">Melaku Teferra</a> was ousted, and his successor removed restrictions on travel out of Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Ethiopian Jews, many by this time waiting in Addis Ababa, began again to arrive in Sudan in large numbers; and the Mossad had trouble evacuating them quickly. Because of the poor conditions in the Sudanese camps, many Ethiopian refugees, both Christian and Jewish, died of disease and hunger. Among these victims, it is estimated that between 2,000 to 5,000 were Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In late 1984, the Sudanese government, following the intervention of the U.S, allowed the emigration of 7,200 Beta Israel refugees to Europe who then went on to Israel. The first of these two immigration waves, between 20 November 1984 and 20 January 1985, was dubbed <a href="/wiki/Operation_Moses" title="Operation Moses">Operation Moses</a> (original name "The Lion of Judah’s Cub") and brought 6,500 Beta Israel to Israel. This operation was followed by <a href="/wiki/Operation_Joshua" title="Operation Joshua">Operation Joshua</a> (also referred to as "Operation Sheba") a few weeks later, which was conducted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S Air Force</a>, and brought the 494 Jews refugees remaining in Sudan to Israel. The second operation was mainly carried out due to the critical intervention and pressure from the U.S.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emigration_via_Addis_Ababa">Emigration via Addis Ababa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Emigration via Addis Ababa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b>1990–1991</b> – After losing Soviet military support following the collapse of Communism in Central and Eastern Europe, the Ethiopian government allowed the emigration of 6,000 Beta Israel members to Israel in small groups, mostly in hope of establishing ties with the U.S, the allies of Israel. Many more Beta Israel members crowded into refugee camps on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, the capital of Ethiopia, to escape the civil war raging in the north of Ethiopia (their region of origin), and await their turn to immigrate to Israel.</li>
<li><b>May 24–25, 1991 (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Solomon" title="Operation Solomon">Operation Solomon</a>)</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isn.ethz.ch_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-isn.ethz.ch-11">[11]</a></sup> – In 1991, the political and economic stability of Ethiopia deteriorated, as rebels mounted attacks against and eventually controlled the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>. Worried about the fate of the Beta Israel during the transition period, the Israeli government, with the help of several private groups, resumed the migration. Over the course of 36 hours, a total of 34 <a href="/wiki/El_Al" title="El Al">El Al</a> <a href="/wiki/Airliner" title="Airliner">passenger planes</a>, with their seats removed to maximize passenger capacity, flew 14,325 Beta Israel non-stop to Israel. Again, the operation was mainly carried out due to intervention and pressure from the U.S.</li>
<li><b>1992–1999</b> – During these years, the <a href="/wiki/Qwara_Province" title="Qwara Province">Qwara</a> Beta Israel immigrated to Israel.</li>
<li><b>1997–present</b> – In 1997, an irregular emigration began of <a href="/wiki/Falash_Mura" title="Falash Mura">Falash Mura</a>, which was and still is mainly subject to political developments in Israel. (see below)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_difficulties_of_the_Falash_Mura_in_immigrating_to_Israel">The difficulties of the Falash Mura in immigrating to Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: The difficulties of the Falash Mura in immigrating to Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1991, the Israeli authorities announced that the emigration of the Beta Israel to Israel was about to conclude, because almost all of the community had been evacuated. Nevertheless, thousands of other Ethiopians began leaving the northern region to take refuge in <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, declaring themselves to be Jewish converts to Christianity and asking to immigrate to Israel. As a result, a new term arose which was used to refer to this group: "<a href="/wiki/Falash_Mura" title="Falash Mura">Falash Mura</a>". The Falash Mura, who weren't part of the Beta Israel communities in Ethiopia, were not recognized as Jews by the Israeli authorities, and were therefore not initially allowed to immigrate to Israel, making them ineligible for Israeli citizenship under Israel's <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Return" title="Law of Return">Law of Return</a>.</p>
<p>As a result, a lively debate has arisen in Israel about the Falash Mura, mainly between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_Israel_community_in_Israel" title="Beta Israel community in Israel">Beta Israel community in Israel</a> and their supporters and those opposed to a potential massive emigration of the Falash Mura people. The government's position on the matter remained quite restrictive, but it has been subject to numerous criticisms, including criticisms by some clerics who want to encourage these people's return to Judaism .</p>
<p>During the 1990s, the Israeli government finally allowed most of those who fled to Addis Ababa to immigrate to Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Some did so through the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Return" title="Law of Return">Law of Return</a>, which allows an Israeli parent of a non-Jew to petition for his/her son or daughter to be allowed to immigrate to Israel. Others were allowed to immigrate to Israel as part of a humanitarian effort.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_(GPO)_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_%28GPO%29_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_%28GPO%29_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_%28GPO%29_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_%28GPO%29_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_%28GPO%29_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_%28GPO%29_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Government_Press_Office_(GPO)_-_PM_Yitzhak_Shamir_Greets_new_immigrants_from_Ethiopia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Israeli PM <a href="/wiki/Yitzhak_Shamir" title="Yitzhak Shamir">Yitzhak Shamir</a> greets new immigrants from Ethiopia, 1991.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Israeli government hoped that admitting these Falash Mura would finally bring emigration from Ethiopia to a close, but instead a new wave of Falash Mura refugees fled to Addis Ababa and demanded the right to immigrate to Israel. This led the Israeli government to harden its position on the matter in the late 1990s.</p>
<p>In February 2003, the Israeli government decided to accept Orthodox religious conversions in Ethiopia of Falash Mura by Israeli Rabbis, after which they can then immigrate to Israel as Jews. Although the new position is more open, and although the Israeli governmental authorities and religious authorities should in theory allow emigration to Israel of most of the Falash Mura wishing to do so (who are now acknowledged to be descendants of the Beta Israel community), in practice, however, that immigration remains slow, and the Israeli government continued to limit, from 2003 to 2006, immigration of Falash Mura to about 300 per month.</p>
<p>In April 2005, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Post" title="Jerusalem Post">Jerusalem Post</a></i> stated that it had conducted a survey in Ethiopia, after which it was concluded that tens of thousands of Falash Mura still lived in rural northern Ethiopia.</p>
<p>On 14 November 2010 the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Israel" title="Cabinet of Israel">Israeli cabinet</a> approved a plan to allow an additional 8,000 Falash Mura to immigrate to Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 16, 2015 the Israeli cabinet unanimously voted in favor of allowing the last group of Falash Mura to immigrate over the next five years but their acceptance will be conditional on a successful Jewish conversion process, according to the Interior Ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In April 2016 they announced a total of 10,300 people would be included in the latest round of Aliyah, over the following 5 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopian_Jews_in_Israel">Ethiopian Jews in Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Ethiopian Jews in Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Jews_in_Israel" title="Ethiopian Jews in Israel">Ethiopian Jews in Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Racism_in_Israel#Beta_Israel" title="Racism in Israel">Racism in Israel § Beta Israel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1123" data-file-width="750" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg/200px-Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg/300px-Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg/400px-Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Israeli_Border_Guard_Police.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Israeli <a href="/wiki/Israel_Border_Police" title="Israel Border Police">Border Policeman</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ethiopian Beta Israel community in Israel today comprises more than 121,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Ethiopian_Population_In_Israel_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Ethiopian_Population_In_Israel-1">[1]</a></sup> Most of this population are the descendants and the immigrants who came to Israel during "<a href="/wiki/Operation_Moses" title="Operation Moses">Operation Moses</a>" (1984) and "<a href="/wiki/Operation_Solomon" title="Operation Solomon">Operation Solomon</a>" (1991).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Civil war and famine in Ethiopia prompted the Israeli government to mount these dramatic rescue operations. The rescues were within the context of Israel's national mission to gather Diaspora Jews and bring them to the Jewish homeland. Some immigration has continued up until the present day. Today 81,000 Ethiopian Israelis were born in Ethiopia, while 38,500 or 32% of the community are native born Israelis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haaretz.com_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-haaretz.com-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over time, the Ethiopian Jews in Israel moved out of the government owned <a href="/wiki/Mobile_home" title="Mobile home">mobile home</a> camps which they initially lived in and settled in various cities and towns throughout Israel, with the encouragement of the Israeli authorities who grant new immigrants generous government loans or low-interest mortgages.</p>
<p>Similarly to other groups of immigrant Jews who made <a href="/wiki/Aliyah" title="Aliyah">aliyah</a> to Israel, the Ethiopian Jews have had to overcome obstacles to integrate into Israeli society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Initially the main challenges faced by the Ethiopian Jewish community in Israel arose from communication difficulties (most of the Ethiopian population could not read nor write in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a>, and many of the older members could not hold a simple conversation in Hebrew), and discrimination, including manifestations of racism, from some parts of Israeli society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Unlike Russian immigrants, many of whom arrived educated and skilled, Ethiopian immigrants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> came from an impoverished agrarian country, and were ill-prepared to work in a developed industrialized country.</p>
<p>Over the years there has been significant progress in the integration of young Beta Israels into Israeli society, primarily resulting from serving in the Israeli Defense Forces alongside other Israelis their age. This has led to an increase in opportunities for Ethiopian Jews after they are discharged from the army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite progress, Ethiopian Jews are still not well assimilated into Israeli-Jewish society. They remain, on average, on a lower economic and educational level than average Israelis. Also, while marriages between Jews of different backgrounds are very common in Israel, marriages between Ethiopians and non-Ethiopians are not very common. According to a 2009 study, 90% of Ethiopian-Israelis – 93% of men and 85% of women, are married to other Ethiopian-Israelis. A survey found that 57% of Israelis consider a daughter marrying an Ethiopian unacceptable and 39% consider a son marrying an Ethiopian to be unacceptable. Barriers to intermarriage have been attributed to sentiments in both the Ethiopian community and Israeli society generally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> A 2011 study showed that only 13% of high school students of Ethiopian origin felt "fully Israeli".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Discrimination and racism against Israeli Ethiopians is still perpetuated. In May 2015 Israeli Ethiopians demonstrated in Tel Aviv and Jerusalem against racism, after a video was released, showing an Israeli soldier of Ethiopian descent that was brutally beaten up by the Israeli police. Interviewed students of Ethiopian origin affirm that they do not feel accepted in Israeli society, due to a very strong discrimination towards them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Converts">Converts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Converts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Falash_Mura">Falash Mura</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Falash Mura">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Falash_Mura" title="Falash Mura">Falash Mura</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="760" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg/250px-1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg/375px-1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg/500px-1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1862BetaIsraelPreach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Missionary <a href="/wiki/Henry_Aaron_Stern" title="Henry Aaron Stern">Henry Aaron Stern</a> preaches Christianity to Beta Israel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Falash Mura is the name given to those of the Beta Israel community in Ethiopia who converted to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> under pressure from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(Christianity)" title="Mission (Christianity)">mission</a> during the 19th century and the 20th century. This term consists of Jews who did not adhere to Jewish law, as well as Jewish converts to Christianity, who did so either voluntarily or <a href="/wiki/Anusim" title="Anusim">who were forced to do so</a>.</p>
<p>Many Ethiopian Jews whose ancestors converted to Christianity have been returning to the practice of Judaism. The Israeli government can thus set quotas on their immigration and make citizenship dependent on their conversion to <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beta_Abraham">Beta Abraham</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Beta Abraham">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Beta_Abraham" title="Beta Abraham">Beta Abraham</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slaves">Slaves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Slaves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaism_and_slavery" title="Judaism and slavery">Judaism and slavery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Ethiopia" title="Slavery in Ethiopia">Slavery in Ethiopia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">Slavery</a> was practiced in Ethiopia as in much of Africa until it was formally abolished in 1942. After the slave was bought by a Jew, he went through <i><a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">Giyur</a></i> and became property of his master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(November 2010)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li>The 2005 Israeli-French film "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go,_Live,_and_Become" title="Go, Live, and Become">Go, Live, and Become</a></i>" (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Cardo, Shofar, David, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;">תחייה ותהייה</span>‎), directed by Romanian-born <a href="/wiki/Radu_Mih%C4%83ileanu" title="Radu Mihăileanu">Radu Mihăileanu</a> focuses on <a href="/wiki/Operation_Moses" title="Operation Moses">Operation Moses</a>. The film tells the story of an Ethiopian Christian child whose mother has him pass as Jewish so he can immigrate to Israel and escape the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1984%E2%80%931985_famine_in_Ethiopia" title="1984–1985 famine in Ethiopia">famine looming in Ethiopia</a>. The film was awarded the 2005 Best Film Award at the <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_International_Film_Festival" title="Copenhagen International Film Festival">Copenhagen International Film Festival</a>.</li>
<li>Several prominent musicians and rappers are of Ethiopian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Memorial_in_Mount_Herzl_in_Jerusalem">National Memorial in Mount Herzl in Jerusalem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: National Memorial in Mount Herzl in Jerusalem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg/250px-EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg/375px-EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg/500px-EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EthiopianJewryMemorial-12A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial to Ethiopian Jews who were murdered on the way to Israel in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Herzl" title="Mount Herzl">Mount Herzl</a> in Jerusalem</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A national memorial to the Ethiopian Jews who died on their way to Israel, located at the National Civil Cemetery of the State of Israel in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Herzl" title="Mount Herzl">Mount Herzl</a> in Jerusalem.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopian_Heritage_Museum">Ethiopian Heritage Museum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Ethiopian Heritage Museum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2009, plans to establish an Ethiopian Heritage Museum dedicated to the heritage and culture of the Ethiopian Jewish community were unveiled in <a href="/wiki/Rehovot" title="Rehovot">Rehovot</a>. The museum will include a model of an Ethiopian village, an artificial stream, a garden, classrooms, an amphitheater, and a memorial to Ethiopian Zionist activists and Ethiopian Jews who died <i>en route</i> to Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caf.C3.A9_Shahor_Hazak">Café Shahor Hazak</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Café Shahor Hazak">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strong_Black_Coffee" title="Strong Black Coffee">Strong Black Coffee</a> ("<i>Café Shahor Hazak</i>"; קפה שחור חזק) is an Ethiopian-Israeli <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">hip hop</a> duo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewishexponent.com_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-jewishexponent.com-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The duo were a nominee for the <a href="/wiki/2015_MTV_Europe_Music_Awards" title="2015 MTV Europe Music Awards">2015 MTV Europe Music Awards</a> <a href="/wiki/MTV_Europe_Music_Award_for_Best_Israeli_Act" title="MTV Europe Music Award for Best Israeli Act">Best Israeli Act</a> award.</p>

<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Steven Kaplan, "Eldad Ha-Dani", in Siegbert von Uhlig, ed., <i>Encyclopaedia Aethiopica: D-Ha</i> (Wiesbaden: Harrassowitz Verlag, 2005), p.252. Medieval travellers' accounts typically are vague in such matters, and are not presented as geographical treatises; moreover, Ethiopians, Sudanese and Somalians do not all know all the tribal languages around them. In earlier times, the different ethnic groups would have been even more insular. In any case, the "Letter of Eldad the Danite" summarized his experiences.</span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beta_Israel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<p><b>General</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Steven Kaplan &amp; Shoshana Ben-Dor (1988). <i>Ethiopian Jewry: An Annotated Bibliography</i>. Ben-Zvi Institute.</li>
<li>Carl Rathjens (1921). <i>Die Juden in Abessinien</i>. W. Gente.</li>
<li>Johann Martin Flad (1869). <i>The Falashas (Jews) of Abyssinia</i>.</li>
<li>James Bruce (1790). <i>Travels to Discover the Source of the Nile</i>.</li>
<li>Henry Aaron Stern (1862).<i>Wanderings among the Falashas in Abyssinia</i>.</li>
<li>Salo Wittmayer Baron (1983). <i>A Social and Religious History of the Jews</i>. Volume XVIII. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0231088558">ISBN 0-231-08855-8</a></li>
</ul>
<p><b>History</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Abbink, Jon (1990). "The Enigma of Esra'el Ethnogenesis: An Anthro-Historical Study". <i>Cahiers d'Etudes africaines</i>, 120, XXX-4, pp. 393–449.</li>
<li>Avner, Yossi (1986). <i>The Jews of Ethiopia: A People in Transition</i>. Beth Hatefutsoth. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0873340396">ISBN 0-87334-039-6</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Budge,_E._A._Wallis" title="Budge, E. A. Wallis">Budge, E. A. Wallis</a> (1932). <a class="external text" href="http://www.sacred-texts.com/chr/kn/" rel="nofollow"><i>The Queen of Sheba and her only son Menelik</i></a>, London.</li>
<li>Herman, Marilyn. "Relating Bet Israel history in its Ethiopian context: Defining, Creating, Constructing Identity". Review article of Quirin (1992) and Kaplan (1992). "Journal of the Anthropological Society of Oxford". Hilary 1996. 27:1. 47-59</li>
<li>Hess, Robert L. (1969). "Toward a History of the Falasha". <i>Eastern African history</i>. Praeger.</li>
<li>Isaac, Ephraim (1974). <i>The Falasha: Black Jews of Ethiopia</i>. Dillard University Scholar Statesman Lecture Series.</li>
<li>Jankowski, Alice (1987). <i>Die Königin von Saba und Salomo</i>, Hamburg, H. Buske Vlg.</li>
<li>Kaplan, Steven (1995). <i>The Beta Israel (Falasha) in Ethiopia: From Earliest Times to the Twentieth Century</i>. New York University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0814746640">ISBN 0-8147-4664-0</a></li>
<li>Kessler, David (1985). <i>The Falashas: the Forgotten Jews of Ethiopia</i>. Schocken Books. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0805207910">ISBN 0-8052-0791-0</a></li>
<li>Kessler, David (1996). <i>The Falashas: a short history of the Ethiopian Jews</i>. Frank Cass. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0714646466">ISBN 0-7146-4646-6</a></li>
<li>Marcus, Louis (1829). "Notice sur l'époque de l'établissement des Juifs dans l'Abyssinie". <i>Journal Asiatique</i>, 3.</li>
<li>Messing, Simon D. (1982). <i>The Story of the Falashas "Black Jews of Ethiopia"</i>. Brooklyn. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0961594691">ISBN 0-9615946-9-1</a></li>
<li>Rapoport, Louis (1980). <i>The Lost Jews: Last of the Ethiopian Falashas</i>. Stein and Day. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0812827201">ISBN 0-8128-2720-1</a></li>
<li>Quirin, James A. (1992). <i>The Evolution of the Ethiopian Jews: a History of the Beta Israel (Falasha) to 1920</i>. University of Pennsylvania Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0812231163">ISBN 0-8122-3116-3</a></li>
<li>Shapiro, Mark (1987). "The Falasha of Ethiopia". <i>The World and I</i>. Washington Times Corp.</li>
<li>Weil, Shalva (2008) 'Jews in Ethiopia', in M.A. Erlich (ed.) Encyclopedia of the Jewish Diaspora, Santa Barbara, USA: ABC CLIO, 2: 467–475.</li>
<li>Weil, Shalva (2011) 'Ethiopian Jews' (165–166) in Judith Baskin (ed.) Cambridge Dictionary of Judaism and Jewish Culture, New York: Cambridge University Press</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Religion</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Jeffrey Lewis Halper (1966). <i>The Falashas: An Analysis of Their History, Religion and Transitional Society</i>. University of Minnesota. 1966</li>
<li>Kay Kaufman Shelemay (1989). <i>Music, Ritual, and Falasha History</i> . Michigan State University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0870132741">ISBN 0-87013-274-1</a></li>
<li>Michael Corinaldi (1988). <i>Jewish Identity: The Case of Ethiopian Jewry</i>. The Magnes Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9652239933">ISBN 965-223-993-3</a></li>
<li>Menahem Valdman (1985). <i>The Jews of Ethiopia: the Beta Israel community</i>. Ami-Shav.</li>
<li>Wolf Leslau (1951). <i>Falasha Anthology</i>. Yale University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0300039271">ISBN 0-300-03927-1</a></li>
<li>Edward Ullendorff (1968). <i>Ethiopia and the Bible</i>. Oxford University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0197260764">ISBN 0-19-726076-4</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Menachem_Elon" title="Menachem Elon">Menachem Elon</a> (1987). <i>The Ethiopian Jews : a case study in the functioning of the Jewish legal system</i>. New York University</li>
<li>Steven Kaplan (1988). "Falasha religion: ancient Judaism or evolving Ethiopian tradition?". <i>Jewish Quarterly Review LXXXIX</i>. Center for Advanced Judaic Studies, University of Pennsylvania.</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Aliyah</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Jerry L. Weaver and Howard M. Lenhoff (2007). <i>Black Jews, Jews, and Other Heroes: How Grassroots Activism Led to the Rescue of the Ethiopian Jews</i>. Gefen Publishing House Ltd. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9789652293657">ISBN 978-965-229-365-7</a></li>
<li>Tudor Parfitt (1986). <i>Operation Moses: the untold story of the secret exodus of the Falasha Jews from Ethiopia</i>. Stein and Day. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0812830598">ISBN 0-8128-3059-8</a></li>
<li>Claire Safran (1987). <i>Secret exodus: the story of Operation Moses</i>. Reader's Digest.</li>
<li>Stephen Spector (2005). <i>Operation Solomon: The Daring Rescue of the Ethiopian Jews</i>. Oxford University Press US. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0195177827">ISBN 0-19-517782-7</a></li>
<li>Shmuel Yilma (1996). <i>From Falasha to Freedom: An Ethiopian Jew's Journey to Jerusalem</i>. Gefen Publishing. House. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9652291692">ISBN 965-229-169-2</a></li>
<li>Alisa Poskanzer (2000). <i>Ethiopian exodus: a practice journal</i>. Gefen Publishing House. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9652292176">ISBN 965-229-217-6</a></li>
<li>Baruch Meiri (2001). <i>The Dream Behind Bars: the Story of the Prisoners of Zion from Ethiopia</i>. Gefen Publishing House. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9652292214">ISBN 965-229-221-4</a></li>
<li>Asher Naim (2003). <i>Saving the lost tribe: the rescue and redemption of the Ethiopian Jews</i>. Ballantine Books. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0345450817">ISBN 0-345-45081-7</a></li>
<li>Micha Odenheimer&amp; Ricki Rosen (2006). <i>Transformations: From Ethiopia to Israel</i>. Reality Check Productions. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9652293776">ISBN 965-229-377-6</a></li>
<li>Gad Shimron (2007). <i>Mossad Exodus: The Daring Undercover Rescue of the Lost Jewish Tribe</i> . Gefen Publishing House. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9652294039">ISBN 965-229-403-9</a></li>
<li>Gadi Ben-Ezer (2002). <i>The Ethiopian Jewish exodus: narratives of the migration journey to Israel, 1977–1985</i>. Routledge. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0415273633">ISBN 0-415-27363-3</a></li>
<li>Weil, Shalva 2012 "Longing for Jerusalem Among the Beta Israel of Ethiopia", in Edith Bruder and Tudor Parfitt (eds.) African Zion: Studies in Black Judaism, Cambridge: Cambridge Scholars Publishing, pp. 204–217.</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Society</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Marilyn Herman (2012). "Gondar's Child: Songs, Honor and Identity Among Ethiopian Jews in Israel". Red Sea Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/156902328X">ISBN 1-56902-328-X</a></li>
<li>Hagar Salamon (1999). <i>The Hyena People: Ethiopian Jews in Christian Ethiopia</i>. University of California Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0520219015">ISBN 0-520-21901-5</a></li>
<li>Kay Kaufman Shelemay &amp; Steven Kaplan (2010). "Creating the Ethiopian Diaspora". <i>Special issue of Diaspora – A Journal of Transnational Studies</i>.</li>
<li>Daniel Summerfield (2003). <i>From Falashas to Ethiopian Jews: the external influences for change c.1860–1960</i>. Routledge. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0700712186">ISBN 0-7007-1218-6</a></li>
<li>Esther Hertzog (1999). <i>Immigrants and bureaucrats: Ethiopians in an Israeli absorption center</i>. Berghahn Books. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/157181941X">ISBN 1-57181-941-X</a></li>
<li>Ruth Karola Westheimer &amp; Steven Kaplan (1992). <i>Surviving salvation: the Ethiopian Jewish family in transition</i>. NYU Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0814792537">ISBN 0-8147-9253-7</a></li>
<li>Tanya Schwarz (2001). <i>Ethiopian Jewish immigrants in Israel: the homeland postponed</i>. Routledge. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0700712380">ISBN 0-7007-1238-0</a></li>
<li>Girma Berhanu (2001). <i>Learning In Context: An Ethnographic Investigation of Meditated Learning Experiences Among Ethiopian Jews in Israel</i>. Goteborg University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9173464112">ISBN 91-7346-411-2</a></li>
<li>Teshome G. Wagaw (1993). <i>For our soul: Ethiopian Jews in Israel</i>. Wayne State University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0814324584">ISBN 0-8143-2458-4</a></li>
<li>Michael Ashkenazi &amp; Alex Weingrod (1987). <i>Ethiopian Jews and Israel</i>. Transaction Publishers. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0887381332">ISBN 0-88738-133-2</a></li>
<li>Tudor Parfitt &amp; Emanuela Trevisan Semi (1999). <i>The Beta Israel in Ethiopia and Israel: studies on Ethiopian Jews</i>. Routledge. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0700710922">ISBN 0-7007-1092-2</a></li>
<li>Tudor Parfitt &amp; Emanuela Trevisan Semi (2005). <i>Jews of Ethiopia: the birth of an elite</i>. Routledge. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0415318386">ISBN 0-415-31838-6</a></li>
<li>Emanuela Trevisan Semi &amp; Shalva Weil (2011). <i>Beta Israel: the Jews of Ethiopia and beyond History, Identity and Borders</i>. Libreria Editrice Cafoscarina. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9788875432867">ISBN 978-88-7543-286-7</a></li>
<li>Weil, Shalva 2012 'I am a teacher and beautiful: the feminization of the teaching profession in the Ethiopian community in Israel', in Pnina Morag- Talmon and Yael Atzmon (eds) Immigrant Women in Israeli Society, Jerusalem: Bialik Institute, pp. 207–223. (Hebrew)</li>
</ul>
